Interpretation: We have n distinct lines. Condition 1: For all positive integers a,b (presumably a,b ∈ ℕ, positive integers) with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. With two sunny lines, we can cover at most two of them. The third would be uncovered. So we need a third line to cover it. But we only have three lines total, one non-sunny and two sunny. So we cannot cover all points. Therefore k=2 seems impossible for n=3. But maybe we can choose a different non-sunny line that covers more points than 3, but max is 3. Now we have found that for n=3, possible k are 0,1,3. But note that k=3 is all sunny, which we constructed. k=0 is all non-sunny (e.g., horizontal lines y=1,2,3). That's 3 lines, but are they all non-sunny? Horizontal lines are not sunny, yes. So that works. So the set of achievable k seems to be all k except 2? But we should also consider whether k can be n (all sunny) for all n≥3. We need to analyze generally. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize k as a function of n. Likely answer: k can be any integer with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except that if k = n-1 (i.e., all but one are sunny) then it's impossible, maybe? Let's explore.

We need to cover all points in S = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. We can choose lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) to cover many points. We need to see what constraints on k arise.

Observation: The points S are arranged in a triangular lattice. The lines that are not sunny (i.e., horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) are natural for covering many points. However, we can also use sunny lines to cover points. But the covering requirement is that every point must be on at least one line. So we can think about the number of points that can be covered by a set of lines of a certain type. The total number of points is large relative to n. But we have n lines, each line can contain at most something like the number of lattice points in S that are collinear. The maximum collinear points in S (with any line) is n, achieved by horizontal lines (y = b for b=1) covering n points, vertical lines (x = a for a=1) covering n points, and slope -1 lines (y = -x + d for d=n+1) covering n points. So non-sunny lines can cover n points. Sunny lines, being not parallel to those directions, can cover at most something like floor((n+1)/2) maybe? Let's compute.

Take a sunny line L with slope not 0,∞,-1. Consider the set of points in S that lie on L. Since L is not horizontal, not vertical, not slope -1, it will intersect the triangle S in a line segment. The maximum number of integer points on such a line within S might be less than n. For example, slope 1 (sunny) yields points where a=b, and a+b ≤ n+1 ⇒ 2a ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So maximum number of points is floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2 (sunny), points (a,2a+c) must be integer; but they are not many. For slope 1/2, points (2a,b) maybe. But we can try to maximize. The line with slope 1 (45°) is natural because it's the "diagonal" that is not excluded. So the maximum number of lattice points on a sunny line in S is at most floor((n+1)/2). But is it possible to have a sunny line that contains more than floor((n+1)/2) points? Consider a line with slope rational p/q (reduced). The integer points on it are spaced by q in x. So to have many points within the triangle, we need q small. But the slope cannot be 0,∞,-1. The maximum number of points on a line with slope not parallel to those three in the triangle is achieved by a line that goes through the "most dense" part, which is along the line a=b, which is slope 1. That's the line that is the "anti-diagonal"? Actually a=b is the line y=x, slope 1. That's not parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1). So it's sunny. Its intersection with S is the set of points where a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 ⇒ 2a ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of points is floor((n+1)/2). Could there be a line with slope 2 that also contains many points? For slope 2, integer points are (a,2a+c). The condition a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. If we take c such that b is integer, but b grows faster. For a=1, b=2+c; a=2, b=4+c; a=3, b=6+c, etc. The sum a+b = a + (2a+c) = 3a + c. To have a+b ≤ n+1, we need 3a + c ≤ n+1. For a large, this fails. So the maximum a is limited. The number of points is roughly (n+1)/3. So less than floor((n+1)/2) for n≥3. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2). Similarly slope 1/2 gives points (a, a/2 + c). For integer points, a must be even. Then a+b = a + (a/2 + c) = 3a/2 + c. So max a is about 2(n+1)/3, but the number of points is about (n+1)/2? Let's compute: For a even, a ≤ something. For n=3, slope 1/2 gives points (2,2?) Actually a=2 gives b=1+c. For c such that b integer. For S, a,b≥1. For slope 1/2, the line through (2,2) is y = (1/2)x + 1.5? That's not integer. But we can adjust. The maximum number of integer points on a line with slope 1/2 is limited by the spacing. The set of integer points on a line with slope 1/2 is arithmetic progression with difference 2 in x. So number of points in S is at most something like floor((n+1)/2) as well? Let's see: For slope 1/2, integer points occur at x = 2k, y = k + c. For S, we need a+b ≤ n+1. If we take c=0, then points (2,1) (a+b=3), (4,2) (6), etc. For n=3, (2,1) is in S, (4,2) is not because a+b=6>4. So only one point. So it's not as dense. So indeed the maximum is achieved by slope 1. So we can say: For any sunny line L, the number of points of S on L is at most floor((n+1)/2). And this bound is attainable (e.g., line y=x). So each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points. Non-sunny lines can cover at most n points. Also note that non-sunny lines could be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, each achieving n points.

Now we have n lines. Suppose we have k sunny lines. Then we have n−k non-sunny lines. The total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most (n−k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2). But we need to cover N points, each at least once. So we must have (n−k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2) ≥ N. However, this is a necessary condition (if the lines are chosen optimally, they could cover less than the maximum possible for each type, but we need at least N points covered). So we need to find k for which there exists a configuration (maybe with lines covering less than maximum). But the inequality is a necessary condition for existence: if the maximum possible coverage (sum of maxima) is less than N, then it's impossible, because even if we place lines optimally, each line can cover at most that many points. But we can also have lines that are not sunny but still cover fewer points; we need to cover N points. So a necessary condition is that the sum of the maxima is ≥ N. But is it sufficient? Not necessarily. However, we can try to construct configurations that achieve the covering with certain k. So we need to determine all k such that there exists a configuration.

Let's compute N = n(n+1)/2. For n≥3, we have floor((n+1)/2) = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Let's denote m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Compute (n−k)*n + k*m ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: (n−k)n + k*m = n^2 - k n + k m = n^2 + k (m - n). Since m ≤ n (for n≥1, m ≤ n). So the coefficient of k is (m - n) ≤ 0. So the sum decreases as k increases. So to satisfy inequality, we need k ≤ some bound? Actually if m - n is negative, then increasing k reduces the sum. So to have sum ≥ N, we need k not too large. For k = n (all sunny), sum = n * m. That's n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2.

Compute n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ vs n(n+1)/2.

Case n even: n = 2t. Then (n+1)/2 = (2t+1)/2 = t + 0.5, floor = t. So m = t. Then n*m = 2t * t = 2t^2. N = n(n+1)/2 = 2t*(2t+1)/2 = t*(2t+1) = 2t^2 + t. So n*m = 2t^2 < 2t^2 + t. So sum < N. So necessary condition fails for k=n (all sunny) when n is even. So if n is even, k=n is impossible. But we already saw for n=3 (odd) k=3 works. So parity matters. For n odd: n = 2t+1. Then (n+1)/2 = (2t+2)/2 = t+1, floor = t+1. So m = t+1. Then n*m = (2t+1)*(t+1) = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. N = n(n+1)/2 = (2t+1)*(2t+2)/2 = (2t+1)*(t+1) = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. So they are equal. So for n odd, n*m = N. So the maximum possible coverage (if we use all sunny lines) would be exactly N. That suggests that if we use all sunny lines, we could potentially cover all points with n lines, provided we can place them so that each sunny line covers exactly floor((n+1)/2) points and these subsets partition S (or cover all points without overlap, but they might overlap). Since sum of maxima equals N, we would need that each sunny line covers exactly its maximum (i.e., the number of points from S on it is exactly m) and that these sets are disjoint (or at least cover all points without gaps). Because if there is overlap, then some points would be covered multiple times and some points might be uncovered (since total capacity is N). But we could also have some sunny lines covering less than maximum, but then we need to compensate with non-sunny lines; but if we use all sunny lines, we cannot have non-sunny lines. So to achieve covering with k=n, we need that the sum of maxima (which is n*m) ≥ N, and also that we can actually achieve covering with n lines. For n odd, n*m = N. So the only chance is that each sunny line covers exactly its maximum (m points) and the covered points are all distinct (i.e., the sets partition S). But can we find n sunny lines such that each contains exactly m points from S, and these n * m points cover all N points with no overlap? That would be a covering where each point lies on exactly one line. That's a partition of S into n subsets of size m, each subset being collinear on a sunny line. But is that possible? For n odd, we can try to construct such a configuration. For n=3, we did that with lines: y=x (slope 1) covers points (1,1),(2,2) (2 points, m=2); line through (2,1)-(1,3) slope -2 covers (2,1),(1,3) (2 points); line through (3,1)-(1,2) slope -0.5 covers (3,1),(1,2) (2 points). Those are 3 lines, each covers 2 points, total 6 points, which is N. Indeed they partition S. So for n=3, it works.

For n=5 (odd), we need to cover points with a+b ≤ 6, a,b ≥ 1. N = 5*6/2 = 15. m = floor((5+1)/2) = 3. So we need 5 sunny lines, each covering 3 points, partitioning S. Is that possible? We need to find 5 lines (sunny) such that each contains exactly 3 points from S, and they are disjoint. The points are in a triangle of size 5. This seems plausible but we need to check whether such a configuration exists. Possibly yes, using lines with slopes 1, -2, -1/2, etc. But we need to determine all k that can be achieved for any n≥3. So we need to analyze necessary and sufficient conditions.

Let's denote:

- Let L_sunny be the set of sunny lines, |L_sunny| = k.
- Let L_non be the set of non-sunny lines, |L_non| = n - k.

For any line L, let p(L) be the number of points of S on L. Then we need ∑_{L} p(L) ≥ N, but that's not sufficient because of overlaps. However, we can consider that each point must be covered at least once, so the sum of p(L) (counting multiplicities) is at least N. But if we have many lines covering many points, overlapping can reduce the efficiency. The inequality ∑ p(L) ≥ N is necessary, but not sufficient (since we could have lines covering many points but they could be all covering the same points, leaving many uncovered). So we need a stronger condition.

But we can try to construct configurations where lines cover disjoint subsets, then ∑ p(L) = N. So if we can achieve that with a certain k, then that k is possible. If the maximum possible ∑ p(L) (by using lines of each type at maximum capacity) is less than N, then impossible. For k=n (all sunny), the sum is n * m. For n odd, n*m = N, so the maximum possible sum (if we use all sunny lines at max) equals N. But we also need to be able to achieve that sum without overlap, i.e., to partition S into n collinear subsets each of size m. For n even, n*m < N, so it's impossible to have all sunny lines. But maybe we can have some non-sunny lines (k < n) and still cover all points. So k can be up to n? For n even, k=n impossible, but perhaps k=n-1 possible? Let's examine.

We need to determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) that can be achieved. Likely answer: k can be any integer with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except that if k = n-1 (or maybe if k = n and n even) then impossible. But we should test with small n: n=3, we found k=0,1,3 possible, k=2 impossible. So k=n-1 =2 impossible. n=4, we can test possibilities. n=4, N = 4*5/2 = 10. m = floor((4+1)/2) = floor(2.5) = 2. So n*m = 4*2 = 8 < 10. So k=n impossible. For k=n-1=3, we have 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The maximum sum of p(L) if we use the non-sunny line at max (4) and sunny lines at max (2 each) gives 4 + 3*2 = 10, which equals N. So the necessary sum condition (max possible sum) is satisfied. So it's plausible that k=3 is achievable for n=4. We need to construct a configuration with 4 lines, exactly 3 sunny, 1 non-sunny, covering all points. Similarly, k=2 (2 sunny, 2 non-sunny) would have max sum 2*4 + 2*2 = 8+4=12 > 10, so necessary condition holds; but is it possible? Probably yes. k=1,0 also possible. So the only questionable values are k=n (all sunny) when n even, and maybe k=n-1 (i.e., all but one sunny) might be impossible for some n? But we need to examine more systematically.

We need to determine all k for which there exists a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points S. The problem is to "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following". So we need to give a description of k in terms of n. For n≥3.

Our initial analysis with necessary condition based on maxima suggests:

- If k = n (all sunny), then necessary condition: n * m ≥ N. Since N = n(n+1)/2. For n odd, equality holds; for n even, inequality fails (n*m < N). So for n even, k=n is impossible. For n odd, k=n is possible? We need to check if a construction exists. For n=3, yes. For n=5, we need to construct. Possibly yes. So we need to prove that for odd n, there exists a configuration with all lines sunny (k=n) covering S. For even n, k=n impossible. Also, for k = n-1, we have one non-sunny line. The necessary condition (max sum) is (n-1)*m + n ≥ N? Actually if we have one non-sunny line, we can use it to cover n points. The remaining (n-1) sunny lines can cover at most (n-1)*m points. The sum of maxima is (n-1)*m + n. But we need to see if this sum ≥ N. Let's compute. For n even: n = 2t. Then m = floor((2t+1)/2) = t. So (n-1)*m + n = (2t-1)*t + 2t = 2t^2 - t + 2t = 2t^2 + t. Suppose we have a line L with slope m (not 0,∞). Let (a,b) and (a',b') be two points on L. Then (b - b')/(a - a') = m. Then the sum (a+b) - (a'+b') = (a - a') + (b - b') = (a - a')(1 + m). If m > -1, then (1+m) > 0, so sum increases as a increases. If m < -1, then (1+m) < 0, so sum decreases. If m = -1, then sum is constant, which is the excluded direction. So capacity of sunny lines exceeds the number of points they must cover by (m-1). That's okay. So capacity not an issue.

But we suspect impossibility because the points not covered by the non-sunny line cannot be covered by the sunny lines due to some combinatorial restrictions. Let's try to analyze more concretely for general odd n.

We have S. Let’s try to see what happens if we take a non-sunny line that is horizontal (y = c). Which c should we choose? The line will cover points with that y. If we choose c = 1, we cover (a,1) for a = 1..n. That's n points. The remaining points are those with b ≥ 2. That's a set T_n of size N - n = n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2. For n odd, n(n-1)/2 is even? Not important. Now, we have n-1 sunny lines. They need to cover T_n. Each sunny line can cover at most m points. So total capacity is (n-1)*m = (n-1)*(n+1)/2 = (n^2-1)/2. Compare with |T_n| = n(n-1)/2. So capacity is (n^2-1)/2. The difference is (n^2-1)/2 - n(n-1)/2 = (n^2-1 - n^2 + n)/2 = (n-1)/2 = m-1. So capacity is larger by m-1. So it's not restrictive.

Now, if we have n-1 sunny lines, they can cover at most (n-1)*(m-1) distinct points from T_n. Since |T_n| = n(n-1)/2. So we need (n-1)*(m-1) ≥ n(n-1)/2 → (m-1) ≥ n/2. For n odd, m-1 = (n-1)/2. But we must consider that the non-sunny line could be of type vertical or slope -1, not necessarily horizontal. The bound we used for T_n (b≥2) depends on the specific non-sunny line's type. But we can apply a similar argument to other families. Let's do a general argument: For odd n, suppose we have n lines, exactly one is not sunny. Let that non-sunny line be L0. We want to show impossibility. We can consider the set of points that are not on L0. They must be covered by sunny lines. But sunny lines have limited coverage. However, we need a bound that holds regardless of which line is chosen. For the horizontal line y = 1, we got a bound on the number of points from T_n (b≥2) that sunny lines can cover. But maybe we can choose L0 to be a vertical line x = 1, then the points not on L0 are those with a ≥ 2. For odd n, similar bound applies: any sunny line can contain at most m-1 points from that region (since the only line with m points is y=x, which includes a=1). But is it possible that a sunny line has m points with a≥2? For slope 1 with offset 0, we have points (1,1) with a=1, so not all a≥2. For offset 1, we have points (1,2),(2,3),..., etc. That's m-1 points overall, and all have a≥1. Among them, those with a=1 are not in the region a≥2. So indeed the maximum number of points with a≥2 on a sunny line is at most m-1 (since if it had m points, it would have to include a point with a=1). Similarly for slope -1 lines. So the bound holds.

Now, we need to compute the size of the region not covered by L0. If L0 is horizontal y = c, then it covers points with that y. The set of points not on L0 is S \ (L0∩S). The number of distinct points not on L0 is N minus the number of points on L0 (distinct). For c = 1, that's N - n. For c = 2, it's N - (n-1) (since y=2 covers a=1..n-1). But we can choose L0 to be the one that covers the most points, to minimize the number of points that must be covered by sunny lines. However, the problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we are allowed to choose any configuration. So for k = n-1, we can try to design a configuration where the one non-sunny line is, say, a horizontal line y = 1 covering n points. Then the remaining points (10) must be covered by n-1 sunny lines. But we argued that for n odd, the maximum points a sunny line can cover from that region is m-1. So with n-1 sunny lines, max coverage is (n-1)*(m-1). But we need to cover |T_n| = n(n-1)/2 points. The inequality fails. Therefore, no such configuration exists. But is it possible to use a non-sunny line that is not horizontal, but still such that the region not covered by it (i.e., points not on that line) has size less than n(n-1)/2, and the sunny lines can cover that region? For example, if L0 is a vertical line x = 1, then points not on L0 are those with a ≥ 2. That set size is N - n = n(n-1)/2. Same size. So bound same. If L0 is a slope -1 line y = -x + (n+1), then points not on L0 are those with a+b ≤ n. That set size is N_n = n(n-1)/2, same size. So all choices of non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) cover n points (maximum), and leave a set of size n(n-1)/2. That's exactly the complement of a maximal anti-diagonal or line. So indeed the set of points not covered by the non-sunny line is of size n(n-1)/2. So the bound is (n-1)*(m-1) for any sunny line covering points from that set. Since m-1 = (n-1)/2, we have (n-1)*(n-1)/2 = (n-1)^2/2. For n ≥ 5 odd, (n-1)^2/2 < n(n-1)/2 = (n^2 - n)/2. So impossible. For n=3, (n-1)^2/2 = (2^2)/2 = 2, and n(n-1)/2 = 3. So inequality fails as well. So k=n-1 is impossible for n=3 too. But we already know that for n=3, k=2 is impossible. So consistent. But we also have a construction with k=n for n=3 (all sunny) that works. So the only possible k for odd n are those ≤ n-2? But also k=n is possible only for n=3. - If n is even, then k can be any integer with 0 ≤ k ≤ n-1.

Let's step back and think about the problem more comprehensively. We have n ≥ 3. We need to determine all k (nonnegative integers) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ n+1. Let's denote S_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

Observations:

- A line is sunny iff it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

- The points S_n are arranged in a triangular lattice. The maximum number of points on a line (any line) that are in S_n is n, achieved by horizontal lines (y = c, with c ≥ 1) covering n points (a=1..n). Similarly vertical lines (x = a) cover n points, and anti-diagonal lines (x+y = d) cover d-1 points; for d = n+1, that's n points. So lines of those three types can cover n points.

- For sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can contain from S_n is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ (let's call m). This is achieved by line y = x (slope 1) and also by line y = x + c? Actually we need to confirm that the maximum is m. Let's prove: For any line L with slope not 0, ∞, -1, consider the integer points on L∩S_n. The line L can be expressed as y = mx + c with m ≠ 0, ∞, -1. The points are integer solutions. The difference in y between two points with integer x is m times the difference in x. For the points to be integer, m must be rational. Write m = p/q in lowest terms. Then the points are spaced by q in x. The number of integer points on L∩S_n is at most something like ⌊(n+1)/q⌋? Not exactly. But we can bound using the fact that a+b is bounded. Another approach: The line L is not parallel to the anti-diagonal, so as a varies, a+b changes linearly with slope (1+m). If m > -1, then a+b increases; if m < -1, a+b decreases. So the line will intersect S_n in a segment. The number of integer points on that segment is at most the number of integer a such that the corresponding b is integer and a+b ≤ n+1. That's like a linear Diophantine equation. The maximum number occurs when m = 1 (since then the line goes through the "dense" part). For m = 1, the integer points are (a,a+c). The condition a ≥ 1, a+c ≥ 1, 2a + c ≤ n+1. The number of such points is something like ⌊(n+1 - c)/2⌋. For c = 0, that's ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For c = 1, it's ⌊n/2⌋, etc. So the maximum is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For other slopes, the number is smaller. So indeed m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Now, we need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines covering S_n, with exactly k sunny lines. Let's denote the set of lines L. We have n lines. Let L_sunny = {L_i} (k of them). Let L_non = {L_j} (n−k). The lines are distinct. The covering condition: S_n ⊆ ⋃_{L} (L∩S_n). The union of the incidences covers all points.

We can attempt to construct configurations with certain k, and also prove that certain k are impossible.

First, note that k = 0 is always possible: take all lines horizontal (y = 1,2,…,n). They are not sunny (since horizontal). That's n lines. They cover all points because each b from 1 to n appears, and for each such b, all a from 1 to n+1-b are covered. So k=0 works.

Now, for k = n, all lines sunny. Then we need to cover S_n with n sunny lines. As argued, if such a configuration exists, then each sunny line must contain exactly m points (since total capacity is n*m, and N = n*m). Moreover, the lines must be disjoint (no overlaps). So we need to find n sunny lines that partition S_n. The only candidate lines that achieve m points are those with slope 1 and offset 0 (i.e., y = x). But is it possible that some other sunny line also has m points? We argued that the maximum is m, and only y = x achieves it. So we need to check whether we can have n lines, all sunny, each with m points. If n is odd, m = (n+1)/2. Then y = x gives m points. So that's one line. To have n lines, we would need n distinct sunny lines, each with m points. Since there is only one such line, impossible. For n even, m = n/2. The line y = x gives (n+2)/2? So k = n is impossible for all n ≥ 3. But wait, we found a construction for n=3 with k=3 (all sunny) that works. But according to this argument, for n=3, m = 2, and the line y = x gives 2 points. With 4 non-sunny lines, we can cover up to 4n - something. The complement size is at least N - 4n + something. So for a given k, we need to see if there exists a configuration (with n lines) such that the union covers S_n. That's a combinatorial design. We can try to find constructions for small k, and prove impossibility for large k. The pattern from n=3 and n=4 suggests that:

- For n=3, possible k are 0,1,3.
- For n=4, possible k are 0,1,2,3.
- For n=5, we need to test. We suspect k=3 is possible, k=4 impossible, k=5 impossible. So possible k are 0,1,2,3. But is k=2 possible? Yes, using e.g., two non-sunny lines and three sunny lines? We need to determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) for which there exists a configuration. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. The solution likely involves analyzing the parity and using constructions based on taking lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) to cover many points, and then covering the remaining points with sunny lines. The trick is that the points in S_n form a triangular lattice, and we can use lines that are parallel to the axes or the anti-diagonal to cover many points, leaving a smaller triangle that can be covered by sunny lines. The number of sunny lines we can afford depends on how many points remain. The question: Given n, we have n lines total. If we choose some lines to be non-sunny, we can cover a certain number of points. The remaining points must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines have limited capacity. So we need to find for which k there exists a choice of lines (some sunny, some non-sunny) such that the union covers S_n.

Let’s denote a = number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - a. We have a non-sunny lines. They are each parallel to one of the three forbidden directions. The union of these a lines can cover at most some number of points. To cover all points, we need that the complement (points not on any non-sunny line) is covered by the sunny lines. The complement is a subset of S_n. The sunny lines are k lines, each can cover at most m points. But we can also have sunny lines covering points that are already covered by non-sunny lines; that's okay. So the necessary condition is that the complement size is ≤ k * m (since each sunny line can cover at most m points, but we need to cover all complement points). However, the complement may be large, but we can also use sunny lines that cover points in the complement and also points on non-sunny lines, but the complement must be covered. So we need |R| ≤ k * m. But also note that the sunny lines might not be able to cover all complement points if the complement points are not collinear in sunny directions. But we can try to design the sunny lines to cover the complement efficiently. The bound |R| ≤ k * m is necessary, but not sufficient. However, we can attempt to construct configurations where the complement is exactly a triangle of size something, and we use a partition into sunny lines. The key is to understand the structure of the complement when we take a set of non-sunny lines.

So many sunny lines. The question: can we find three sunny lines such that each point is on at least one line, and lines are distinct? Actually we want to find a transformation T such that T(complement) = S_3. The complement points are (a,b) with b ≥ n-2. If we apply T(a,b) = (a, b - (n-2) + 1) = (a, b - n + 3). Then b - n + 3 ranges from 1 to 3. The points become:

b = n-2 → b' = 1 → (1,1),(2,1),(3,1)

That's exactly S_3. So T is a translation that maps the complement to S_3. Great. So we can take the three sunny lines that partition S_3 (the construction for n=3) and then translate them by (0, n-2) to cover the complement. Thus, we have constructions for k=0,1,3.

Now, we need to prove impossibility for all other k. Since k can be any integer between 0 and n inclusive. We need to show that if k is not 0,1,3 then no configuration exists. That includes k=2, and k≥4. But we also need to consider k = n when n ≥ 4, which is ≥4, so covered. So we need to prove that for any n ≥ 3, there is no configuration with exactly k sunny lines where k = 2 or k ≥ 4. However, is k=4 possible for some n? For n=4, k=4 is impossible. For n=5, k=4 impossible. For n=6, k=4 impossible. For n=7, k=4 impossible. So seems plausible. But we must be careful: Could there be a configuration with k=4 where not all sunny lines are used to cover the complement in a way that bypasses the bound? The impossibility proof should be based on a counting argument or a structural property. Since we have many lines, we can try to prove that the total number of points that can be covered by sunny lines is at most something like (k*m) but that's not enough because we can also use non-sunny lines. But we need to show that if k ≥ 4, then regardless of how we choose the lines, we cannot cover all points. So we need a necessary condition that forces k ≤ 3. But we already have k=3 is possible. So we need to prove that if k ≥ 4, then it's impossible. But is it possible that k ≥ 4 but we use many non-sunny lines that are not all of the same type? The total number of lines is n. If k ≥ 4, then the number of non-sunny lines is n - k. That's at most n - 4. So we have at most n-4 non-sunny lines. They can cover at most some number of points. We need to see whether it's possible that those non-sunny lines cover enough points so that the remaining points can be covered by k sunny lines. The maximum points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is limited by their number. But maybe we can choose non-sunny lines to cover almost all points, leaving a small set that can be covered by k sunny lines. The best we can do is to use as many non-sunny lines as possible to maximize coverage. Since n - k could be small if k is large, we cannot cover many points with non-sunny lines. For example, if k = n (all sunny), then we have 0 non-sunny lines, and we need to cover all points with n sunny lines. We already argued that's impossible for n≥4. For n=3, it's possible. So we need to prove impossibility for all k ≥ 2 except k=3, but also for k=2. Let's try to derive a general bound.

Let a = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - a. The union of a non-sunny lines can cover at most a*n points (since each line covers at most n points). However, they may overlap, so the actual number of distinct points covered is at most a*n. But we need to cover N points. The sunny lines can cover at most k*m points. So a necessary condition is a*n + k*m ≥ N. Since N = n(n+1)/2. Let's compute:

a*n + k*m = a*n + (n - a)*m = a*n + n*m - a*m = n*m + a*(n - m). Since m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n odd, m = (n+1)/2, n - m = n - (n+1)/2 = (n-1)/2. So a*(n - m) = a*(n-1)/2. For n even, m = n/2, n - m = n/2. So a*(n - m) = a*n/2.

Now, the necessary condition becomes:

n*m + a*(n - m) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

But n*m = n*⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n odd, n*m = n*(n+1)/2 = N. So the left-hand side is N + a*(n-1)/2. That's > N. So the condition is automatically satisfied for any a≥0. So that doesn't restrict k. For n even, n*m = n*(n/2) = n^2/2. N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So n*m = N - n/2. So the inequality becomes N - n/2 + a*(n/2) ≥ N → a*(n/2) ≥ n/2 → a ≥ 1. So if a ≥ 1 (i.e., at least one non-sunny line), then condition holds. For a = 0 (all sunny), inequality fails. So for n even, all sunny impossible. That's consistent. But for n even, k can be as large as n-1 (a=1) which is possible? We saw n=4 (even) had k=3 possible (a=1). For n=6, a=1 (k=5) failed; but the inequality allowed it. So the inequality is not sufficient. So we need a stronger argument.

The problem is essentially about covering the set of lattice points in a triangle with lines. We have a construction for k=3. We suspect that k=2 is impossible. Let's try to prove that for any n≥3, any configuration with exactly k sunny lines must have k ≤ 3. But we already have k=3 is possible. So we need to prove that k ≥ 4 is impossible. However, maybe k=4 is possible for some n if we use a different combination of lines. Let's test n=5 with k=4 (1 non-sunny). We argued it's impossible. For n=6, k=4 (2 non-sunny) impossible. For n=7, k=4 (3 non-sunny) impossible. So it seems never. But we need a proof.

One approach: Show that any line that is not sunny (i.e., parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0) can cover at most n points. The total number of points N is about n^2/2. If we have many sunny lines, they can cover many points, but they are limited to m per line. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k*m points. The remaining points must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are a = n - k lines. They can cover at most a*n points. So necessary condition: (n - k)*n + k*m ≥ N. This is not restrictive. But we can also consider that the points covered by sunny lines are those that lie on lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1. The structure of S_n is such that the maximum number of points that can be covered by a set of k sunny lines is at most something like k*m, but that's not a bound on distinct points because they might overlap. However, we can try to prove that if k ≥ 4, then regardless of how we choose the lines, the number of points that can be covered by the union of the sunny lines is at most something like (k-1)*m + something? That doesn't make sense.

Instead, we should attempt a combinatorial double-counting argument based on anti-diagonals. Consider the sets A_d = { (a,b) ∈ S_n : a+b = d } for d = 2,…,n+1. The size of A_d is d-1. A line L that is not parallel to x+y=0 intersects each A_d in at most one point. So if we have k sunny lines, the total number of incidences (points on sunny lines) is at most k * (number of anti-diagonals they intersect). But we can bound the maximum number of anti-diagonals that a sunny line can intersect within S_n. For a line L with slope not -1, as we move along L, a+b changes linearly, so the line will intersect some anti-diagonals. The number of anti-diagonals intersected is the length of the segment within S_n. The maximum possible number is n (when the line is horizontal or vertical, but those are not sunny). For sunny lines, the maximum number of anti-diagonals intersected is m? Actually consider y = x. For n odd, it intersects anti-diagonals with d = 2,…,n+1? The points on y = x have a+b = 2a. So d is even. The number of such points is (n+1)/2, which is m. So that's m. So a sunny line can intersect at most m anti-diagonals. But is it possible that a sunny line intersects more than m anti-diagonals? For a line with slope, say, 2, the sum a+b = 3a + c. As a increases, the sum increases by 3. The number of integer a such that the point lies in S_n is something like floor((n+1 - c)/3). That's at most about n/3. So less than m. So indeed, the maximum is m. So each sunny line can cover at most m points. That's known.

Now, suppose we have a configuration with exactly k sunny lines. Let R be the set of points not on any of the non-sunny lines. Since the non-sunny lines are at most a = n - k, they can cover at most a*n points, but they might not cover all points. The points in R must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most k*m points overall, but they may also cover points that are on non-sunny lines. The key is to show that if k ≥ 4, then it's impossible to cover all points, maybe because the maximum number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines is less than N - (something). But that's not true as we saw for k=3 (k*m = 3*⌊(n+1)/2⌋). For n=5, 3*3=9, N=15. So 9 < 15. But we still covered all points because we used 2 non-sunny lines covering the rest. So the sunny lines don't need to cover all points. So we need to combine both types. So the impossibility for k≥4 must rely on some structural property: maybe that if you have many sunny lines, then the non-sunny lines are few, and the points that are not covered by non-sunny lines are too many to be covered by the sunny lines because each sunny line can cover at most m points, and the complement size is about N - (n-k)*n, which for small a is large. But for k large, a is small, so complement size is large, but the sunny lines have limited capacity. So we can try to prove that |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| ≤ k*m, and then show that if k ≥ 4, then this inequality fails because |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| is at least something like N - (n-k)*n (since each non-sunny line covers at most n points). But careful: The union of non-sunny lines might have size up to (n-k)*n, but it could be less due to overlaps. So the complement size is at least N - (n-k)*n. So a necessary condition is N - (n-k)*n ≤ k*m. That's the same inequality as before with a = n - k. So it's not restrictive for odd n? Let's compute for n odd, N = n*(n+1)/2, m = (n+1)/2. Then N - (n-k)*n = n(n+1)/2 - n(n-k) = n(n+1)/2 - n^2 + nk = (n^2 + n - 2n^2 + 2nk)/2 = (-n^2 + n + 2nk)/2 = (n( -n + 1 + 2k))/2. The inequality N - (n-k)*n ≤ k*m = k*(n+1)/2 becomes:

(n( -n + 1 + 2k))/2 ≤ k*(n+1)/2 → multiply by 2: n( -n + 1 + 2k) ≤ k(n+1) → -n^2 + n + 2nk ≤ kn + k → bring terms: -n^2 + n + 2nk - kn ≤ k → -n^2 + n + nk ≤ k → nk - k ≤ n^2 - n → k(n - 1) ≤ n(n-1) → k ≤ n. That's always true. So that doesn't restrict. But we need a stronger bound: The complement's size is at least N - (n-k)*n, but could be larger if the non-sunny lines overlap a lot. However, to get a necessary condition for existence, we need that there exists a choice of lines (some sunny, some non-sunny) such that the union covers S_n. Let's compute that inequality.

N - (n - k)*n ≤ k*m ?

We want to see if this inequality fails. Compute N - (n - k)*n = n(n+1)/2 - n(n - k) = n(n+1)/2 - n^2 + nk = (n^2 + n - 2n^2 + 2nk)/2 = (-n^2 + n + 2nk)/2 = n( -n + 1 + 2k)/2.

Now, k*m = k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n odd, m = (n+1)/2, so k*m = k*(n+1)/2. The inequality N - (n - k)*n ≤ k*m becomes:

We have n lines. The covering condition is that every point in S must lie on at least one of the lines. The lines are distinct. We want to maximize/minimize the number of sunny lines given that we can choose lines arbitrarily. But the problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following". So we need to characterize k for which a configuration exists. Likely k can be any integer between some lower bound and n, maybe excluding some values. We need to find constraints.

First, note that lines that are not sunny (i.e., horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) can be used to cover many points. However, there is a limit: The set S is a triangular grid. For horizontal lines, each horizontal line y = b (with b≥1) will cover points (a,b) for a=1,..., n+1-b. So the maximum number of points a horizontal line can cover is when b is small. For b=1, covers points (1,1) to (n,1) (since a≤n). That's n points. For b=2, covers (1,2) to (n-1,2) (if n≥2), that's n-1 points. So horizontal lines cover up to n points. Similarly vertical lines cover up to n points. Slope -1 lines: y = -x + d, for d such that there exist points (a,b) with a+b = d, and a,b≥1, so d≥2. For d from 2 to n+1, the line covers points (a,d-a) with a≥1, d-a≥1 => 1 ≤ a ≤ d-1. So number of points covered is d-1. So the maximum is when d = n+1, covering n points. So lines of these three types can cover up to n points each.

Now we have n lines. If we use only non-sunny lines, we have up to n lines covering points. But can we cover all N points with n lines? Possibly yes if we choose appropriately. However, we also need exactly k sunny lines, so some lines are sunny. The question: For which k does there exist a set of n lines (distinct) with exactly k sunny lines satisfying coverage. So we need to find all k for which such a set exists.

We can attempt to construct configurations with many sunny lines, and also with few sunny lines. But we must be careful: The covering condition imposes that all points in S must be covered. So if we have too many sunny lines, they may not cover enough points because sunny lines are not aligned with the grid, but we can still cover points by placing them appropriately. But we also have n lines, and we can choose lines that are not sunny to cover many points, but we are limited to n lines total. So we need to see if we can cover all points with n lines, regardless of sunny count, but the condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" means that we can decide which lines are sunny. So we can try to maximize the number of sunny lines (i.e., make all lines sunny) but is it possible? Let's check: With n sunny lines, all lines are sunny, meaning they are not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. They have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1. Could we cover all points with such lines? Possibly we could use lines that pass through many points, but with slopes not those three, they will intersect each point at most once, but we can design them to cover many points if they pass through many lattice points. However, a line with a slope that is not 0, ∞, or -1 can still pass through many integer lattice points if its slope is rational and it goes through integer points with some offset. For example, slope 1/2 (line y = (1/2)x + c) will pass through integer points if c is appropriately chosen. But does it pass through many points of S? The points S are all integer points with a+b ≤ n+1, so they are arranged in a triangular grid. A line with slope 1/2 will intersect the grid at points where a is even and b = a/2 + c, but they may not align well. In general, a line can pass through at most something like O(n) points in such a triangle, but we can try to cover all points with n lines (which is less than N). But we have n lines, and we need to cover N points. If we use all sunny lines, then each line can cover many points, but is it possible to cover all N points with n lines? Possibly if we choose lines that are not sunny but we are forced to have sunny lines? But we can also choose lines that are not sunny; they are more efficient. So the maximum number of sunny lines we could have is n (all sunny). But is that feasible? Let's test with small n. n=3. We need 3 lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤4. Points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(1,2),(2,2),(1,3). That's 6 points. With 3 lines, can we cover all 6 points? If we use three horizontal lines y=1,2,3? But y=3 covers only (1,3). y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). That's three lines covering all points. But are they sunny? Horizontal lines are not sunny, so they are not sunny. So k=0 works. Could we have k=3? That would require all three lines sunny. But we can try to construct 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. Is it possible? Let's attempt. We need to cover all points. Perhaps we can use lines with slopes like 1/2, 2, -2, etc. But we must ensure that each point lies on at least one of them. With 3 lines, they can cover at most 3 * (max points per line) points. The maximum points per line (if it's not sunny) is n=3, but for sunny lines, the maximum might be less because they cannot be horizontal/vertical/diagonal. However, we can still design a sunny line to pass through many integer points. For example, a line with slope 1 (not allowed because slope 1 is parallel to x+y=0? Actually x+y=0 has slope -1. But we should also consider whether k can be n (all sunny) for all n≥3. We need to analyze generally. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize k as a function of n. Likely answer: k can be any integer with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except that if k = n-1 (i.e., all but one are sunny) then it's impossible, maybe? We need to cover all points in S = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. We can choose lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) to cover many points. We need to see what constraints on k arise.

Observation: The points S are arranged in a triangular lattice. The lines that are not sunny (i.e., horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) are natural for covering many points. However, we can also use sunny lines to cover points. But the covering requirement is that every point must be on at least one line. So we can think about the number of points that can be covered by a set of lines of a certain type. The total number of points is large relative to n. But we have n lines, each line can contain at most something like the number of lattice points in S that are collinear. The maximum collinear points in S (with any line) is n, achieved by horizontal lines (y = b for b=1) covering n points, vertical lines (x = a for a=1) covering n points, and slope -1 lines (y = -x + d for d=n+1) covering n points. So non-sunny lines can cover n points. Sunny lines, being not parallel to those directions, can cover at most something like floor((n+1)/2) maybe? Let's compute.

Take a sunny line L with slope not 0,∞,-1. Consider the set of points in S that lie on L. Since L is not horizontal, not vertical, not slope -1, it will intersect the triangle S in a line segment. The maximum number of integer points on such a line within S might be less than n. For example, slope 1 (sunny) yields points where a=b, and a+b ≤ n+1 ⇒ 2a ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So maximum number of points is floor((n+1)/2). For slope 2 (sunny), points (a,2a+c) must be integer; but they are not many. For slope 1/2, points (2a,b) maybe. But we can try to maximize. The line with slope 1 (45°) is natural because it's the "diagonal" that is not excluded. So the maximum number of lattice points on a sunny line in S is at most floor((n+1)/2). But is it possible to have a sunny line that contains more than floor((n+1)/2) points? Consider a line with slope rational p/q (reduced). The integer points on it are spaced by q in x. So to have many points within the triangle, we need q small. But the slope cannot be 0,∞,-1. The maximum number of points on a line with slope not parallel to those three in the triangle is achieved by a line that goes through the "most dense" part, which is along the line a=b, which is slope 1. That's the line that is the "anti-diagonal"? Actually a=b is the line y=x, slope 1. That's not parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1). So it's sunny. Its intersection with S is the set of points where a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 ⇒ 2a ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of points is floor((n+1)/2). Could there be a line with slope 2 that also contains many points? For slope 2, integer points are (a,2a+c). The condition a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. If we take c such that b is integer, but b grows faster. For a=1, b=2+c; a=2, b=4+c; a=3, b=6+c, etc. The sum a+b = a + (2a+c) = 3a + c. To have a+b ≤ n+1, we need 3a + c ≤ n+1. For a large, this fails. So the maximum a is limited. The number of points is roughly (n+1)/3. So less than floor((n+1)/2) for n≥3. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2). Similarly slope 1/2 gives points (a, a/2 + c). For n=3, slope 1/2 gives points (2,2?) Actually a=2 gives b=1+c. For c such that b integer. For S, a,b≥1. For slope 1/2, the line through (2,2) is y = (1/2)x + 1.5? That's not integer. But we can adjust. The maximum number of integer points on a line with slope 1/2 is limited by the spacing. The set of integer points on a line with slope 1/2 is arithmetic progression with difference 2 in x. So number of points in S is at most something like floor((n+1)/2) as well? Let's see: For slope 1/2, integer points occur at x = 2k, y = k + c. For S, we need a+b ≤ n+1. If we take c=0, then points (2,1) (a+b=3), (4,2) (6), etc. For n=3, (2,1) is in S, (4,2) is not because a+b=6>4. So only one point. So it's not as dense. So indeed the maximum is achieved by slope 1. So we can say: For any sunny line L, the number of points of S on L is at most floor((n+1)/2). And this bound is attainable (e.g., line y=x). So each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points. Non-sunny lines can cover at most n points. Also note that non-sunny lines could be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, each achieving n points.

Now we have n lines. Suppose we have k sunny lines. Then we have n−k non-sunny lines. The total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most (n−k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2). But we need to cover N points, each at least once. So we must have (n−k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2) ≥ N. However, this is a necessary condition (if the lines are chosen optimally, they could cover less than the maximum possible for each type, but we need at least N points covered). So we need to find k for which there exists a configuration (maybe with lines covering less than maximum). But the inequality is a necessary condition for existence: if the maximum possible coverage (sum of maxima) is less than N, then it's impossible, because even if we place lines optimally, each line can cover at most that many points. But we can also have lines that are not sunny but still cover fewer points; we need to cover N points. So a necessary condition is that the sum of the maxima is ≥ N. But is it sufficient? Not necessarily. However, we can try to construct configurations that achieve the covering with certain k. So we need to determine all k such that there exists a configuration.

Let's compute N = n(n+1)/2. For n≥3, we have floor((n+1)/2) = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Let's denote m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Compute (n−k)*n + k*m ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: (n−k)n + k*m = n^2 - k n + k m = n^2 + k (m - n). Since m ≤ n (for n≥1, m ≤ n). So the coefficient of k is (m - n) ≤ 0. So the sum decreases as k increases. So to satisfy inequality, we need k ≤ some bound? Actually if m - n is negative, then increasing k reduces the sum. So to have sum ≥ N, we need k not too large. For k = n (all sunny), sum = n * m. That's n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2.

Compute n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ vs n(n+1)/2.

Case n even: n = 2t. Then (n+1)/2 = (2t+1)/2 = t + 0.5, floor = t. So m = t. Then n*m = 2t * t = 2t^2. N = n(n+1)/2 = 2t*(2t+1)/2 = t*(2t+1) = 2t^2 + t. So n*m = 2t^2 < 2t^2 + t. So sum < N. So necessary condition fails for k=n (all sunny) when n is even. So if n is even, k=n is impossible. But we already saw for n=3 (odd) k=3 works. So parity matters. For n odd: n = 2t+1. Then (n+1)/2 = (2t+2)/2 = t+1, floor = t+1. So m = t+1. Then n*m = (2t+1)*(t+1) = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. N = n(n+1)/2 = (2t+1)*(2t+2)/2 = (2t+1)*(t+1) = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. So they are equal. So for n odd, n*m = N. So the maximum possible coverage (if we use all sunny lines) would be exactly N. That suggests that if we use all sunny lines, we could potentially cover all points with n lines, provided we can place them so that each sunny line covers exactly floor((n+1)/2) points and these subsets partition S (or cover all points without overlap, but they might overlap). Since sum of maxima equals N, we would need that each sunny line covers exactly its maximum (i.e., the number of points from S on it is exactly m) and that these sets are disjoint (or at least cover all points without gaps). Because if there is overlap, then some points would be covered multiple times and some points might be uncovered (since total capacity is N). But we could also have some sunny lines covering less than maximum, but then we need to compensate with non-sunny lines; but if we use all sunny lines, we cannot have non-sunny lines. So to achieve covering with k=n, we need that the sum of maxima (which is n*m) ≥ N, and also that we can actually achieve covering with n lines. For n odd, n*m = N. So the only chance is that each sunny line covers exactly its maximum (m points) and the covered points are all distinct (i.e., the sets partition S). But can we find n sunny lines such that each contains exactly m points from S, and these n * m points cover all N points with no overlap? That would be a covering where each point lies on exactly one line. That's a partition of S into n subsets of size m, each subset being collinear on a sunny line. But is that possible? For n odd, we can try to construct such a configuration. Case 1: n is even, n = 2t. Then m = t. So (n−k)*n + k*m = (2t−k)*(2t) + k*t = 4t^2 - 2t k + t k = 4t^2 - t k. N = (2t)*(2t+1)/2 = t(2t+1) = 2t^2 + t. So inequality: 4t^2 - t k ≥ 2t^2 + t → 2t^2 - t k ≥ t → divide by t (t≥1? n≥3 => t≥2? Actually n=2t≥3 => t≥2). So 2t - k ≥ 1 → k ≤ 2t - 1 = n - 1. So if k = n (all sunny), inequality fails (k=n => k > n-1). So k = n impossible. If k = n-1, inequality becomes 4t^2 - t (n-1) = 4t^2 - t (2t-1) = 4t^2 - 2t^2 + t = 2t^2 + t = N, equality holds. So k = n-1 is allowed by the inequality. So necessary condition: k ≤ n-1. So for even n, k can be at most n-1. That is, all sunny impossible. So possible k are 0 ≤ k ≤ n-1, but we also need to check if any of those are impossible due to other constraints (like k = n-1 might be impossible? But we found a construction for n=4 with k=3). For n=2 (but n≥3), so even n≥4. So we need to see if for even n≥4, all k from 0 to n-1 are possible. That seems plausible. However, we should also check n=4, k=2,1,0 are possible. So far consistent.

Case 2: n is odd, n = 2t+1. Then m = t+1. N = (2t+1)*(t+1) = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. Compute (n−k)*n + k*m = (2t+1 - k)*(2t+1) + k*(t+1). Expand: = (2t+1)^2 - k(2t+1) + k(t+1) = (2t+1)^2 + k( (t+1) - (2t+1) ) = (2t+1)^2 + k( t+1 -2t -1 ) = (2t+1)^2 + k( -t ) = (2t+1)^2 - t k. (2t+1)^2 = 4t^2 + 4t + 1. So sum = 4t^2 + 4t + 1 - t k. N = 2t^2 + 3t + 1. So inequality: 4t^2 + 4t + 1 - t k ≥ 2t^2 + 3t + 1 → 2t^2 + t - t k ≥ 0 → 2t^2 + t ≥ t k → divide by t (t≥1 for n≥3 => t≥1). So 2t + 1 ≥ k. Since k ≤ n = 2t+1, we get k ≤ 2t+1 = n. So the inequality is satisfied for all k ≤ n (since it's a bound k ≤ 2t+1). For k = n, inequality becomes 2t^2 + t - t n = 2t^2 + t - t(2t+1) = 2t^2 + t - 2t^2 - t = 0, equality. So k=n (all sunny) is allowed by the inequality. So necessary condition from sum of maxima is not restrictive for odd n. So for odd n, all k up to n are potentially possible. But we saw for n=3, k=2 (n-1) is impossible, which contradicts the idea that all k ≤ n are possible. So there is an additional restriction beyond the sum-of-maxima inequality. Let's try to analyze the covering problem more deeply. The points S are all integer points in a triangle. Consider the set of lines that are not sunny. They are horizontal, vertical, and slope -1. Let's consider the case n is odd, n ≥ 3. Suppose we have n lines, exactly one of them is not sunny. Let that line be L0. L0 is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Each sunny line can cover at most m = (n+1)/2 points (since n odd). So total capacity of sunny lines is (n−1)*m = (n−1)*(n+1)/2 = (n^2 - 1)/2. N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. The difference N - (n^2 - 1)/2 = (n^2 + n - n^2 + 1)/2 = (n + 1)/2 = m. So the sunny lines can cover at most (n^2 - 1)/2 points, but we need to cover N points. The non-sunny line L0 can cover at most n points, but those may overlap with sunny lines. The total distinct points covered by all lines must be N. So we need that the sunny lines together with L0 cover all points. The sunny lines can cover at most (n^2 - 1)/2 distinct points, but they might cover less if there are overlaps among themselves. However, even if they cover all points except those covered by L0, we need that the union covers N. The maximum possible distinct points covered by sunny lines is (n^2 - 1)/2. Since N = (n^2 + n)/2, the difference is m. So if the sunny lines cover at most (n^2 - 1)/2 distinct points, then the remaining points (at least N - (n^2 - 1)/2 = m) must be covered by L0. But L0 can cover at most n points, which is more than m. So it's possible that L0 covers those m points, and the sunny lines cover the rest. However, we also need to consider that the sunny lines might cover some of the points that L0 also covers, but that doesn't hurt. The key is: can the sunny lines cover all points except some set of size m, and L0 covers those m points? That would be okay. But we need to see whether such a configuration exists. The necessary condition from capacity is that (n−1)*m + n ≥ N, which holds (equality when k = n−1? Let's compute: (n−1)*m + n = (n−1)*(n+1)/2 + n = (n^2 - 1)/2 + n = (n^2 - 1 + 2n)/2 = (n^2 + 2n - 1)/2. N = (n^2 + n)/2. So inequality becomes (n^2 + 2n - 1)/2 ≥ (n^2 + n)/2 → n - 1 ≥ 0 → n ≥ 1, true. So it's not restrictive. So capacity doesn't forbid.

But we need a more refined argument. Perhaps we can show that any line that is not sunny (i.e., parallel to those three) must cover a certain set of points that is not disjoint from the points covered by sunny lines, but that's okay. The problem is to cover all points. Maybe we can prove that if you have only one non-sunny line, then the points on that line (or the points that are not covered by it) cannot be covered by the sunny lines because the sunny lines have limited "coverage" in some sense. Let's think about the structure of S. The points are arranged in a triangle. The anti-diagonals (a+b = d) have d-1 points. A sunny line can intersect each anti-diagonal at most once. So the total number of points on a sunny line is the number of anti-diagonals it passes through. The maximum number of anti-diagonals intersected by a line that is not parallel to x+y=0 is something like: if the line has slope not -1, then as a increases, a+b increases or decreases linearly. The line will cross anti-diagonals at certain intervals. The number of anti-diagonals intersected is at most something like the number of integer steps. We can relate m to the maximum number of points on a sunny line. That's already established.

Now, if we have n-1 sunny lines, they can cover at most (n-1)*m distinct points. Since N = n*m, there are m points left uncovered by the sunny lines. But we also have one non-sunny line L0. If L0 can cover those m points, then we are done. However, is it always possible that L0 covers those m points? L0 is a line parallel to one of the three directions. The points it can cover are those that lie on a line of that type (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). The set of points covered by L0 is a subset of S of size at most n. But we need to cover exactly m points that are not covered by sunny lines. We can try to choose L0 appropriately. The problem asks: for a given n, does there exist some configuration (with exactly k sunny lines) satisfying the coverage. So we can choose which lines are sunny, and we can choose their equations. So we are free to design the configuration. So for k = n-1, we can try to construct a configuration with n-1 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. We need to see whether such a configuration exists. Consider the set of points S. Let’s denote for each integer d from 2 to n+1, the anti-diagonal A_d = { (a,b) ∈ S : a+b = d }. Its size is d-1. What about lines with slope 1/2? Points (a, a/2 + c). For a even, a=2 gives b=1+c, sum=3+c ≤8 => c ≤5. a=4 gives b=2+c, sum=6+c ≤8 => c ≤2. a=6 gives b=3+c, sum=9+c ≤8 impossible. So only a=2 and a=4 can be in S. That's 2 points. So not 3.

Thus, only y=x gives 3 points in R. So with 5 sunny lines, the maximum distinct points we can cover in R is 3 + 4*2 = 11. That's insufficient. Therefore, for n=7, k = n-2 = 5 is impossible. So our earlier speculation that k = n-2 is always possible for odd n≥5 is false. The construction we gave for n=5 worked because we used 2 non-sunny lines (x=1,y=1), leaving R of size 6, and then 3 sunny lines covering those 6 points. For n=7, we would need to cover R of size 15 with 5 sunny lines, which seems impossible. So maybe the maximum k for odd n≥5 is something like n-3? Let's test n=5, max k is 3 (n-2). For n=7, maybe max k is 4? Let's test with n=7, k=4 (i.e., 4 sunny lines). That would be 3 non-sunny lines. With 3 non-sunny lines, we can cover up to 3*7 = 21 points, but they may overlap. The complement size is at least N - 3n + something. The minimal complement size occurs when non-sunny lines are chosen to cover as many distinct points as possible. With 3 lines, we can cover at most 3n - 3? Actually three lines can be chosen such that they are all different directions, maybe covering up to 3n - 2? But let's compute: If we use y=1 (covers n), x=1 (covers n), and y = -x + (n+1) (covers n). These three lines intersect pairwise. The union size can be up to 3n - 3? Actually we can compute maximum distinct points covered by three lines each of type that covers n points. The lines are: L1: y=1 (points (a,1) for a=1..n). L2: x=1 (points (1,b) for b=1..n). L3: y = -x + (n+1) (points with a+b=n+1). These three lines intersect at points: L1∩L2 = (1,1). L1∩L3 = (n,1). L2∩L3 = (1,n). Overlap is 3 points? Actually they might have pairwise intersections, and also possible triple intersection? (1,1) is on L1,L2, but not on L3 because 1+1=2 ≠ n+1. (n,1) is on L1,L3, not on L2. (1,n) is on L2,L3, not on L1. So three distinct intersections. So total distinct points covered = n + n + n - 3 = 3n - 3. That's the maximum for three lines of those types (if they are chosen as those). For n=7, that's 21 - 3 = 18. Then complement size = N - 18 = 28 - 18 = 10. So with 4 sunny lines, we need to cover 10 points. The maximum points a sunny line can have in that complement is at most m = 4. 4*4 = 16 > 10, so capacity okay. But we need to see if we can actually cover those 10 points with 4 sunny lines. That seems plausible. So maybe k = n-3 is possible for odd n≥5. But we need a systematic approach.

Let's step back and think about the problem more comprehensively. We have n ≥ 3. We need to determine all k (nonnegative integers) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ n+1. Let's denote S_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

Observations:

- A line is sunny iff it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

- The points S_n are arranged in a triangular lattice. The maximum number of points on a line (any line) that are in S_n is n, achieved by horizontal lines (y = c, with c ≥ 1) covering n points (a=1..n). Similarly vertical lines (x = a) cover n points, and anti-diagonal lines (x+y = d) cover d-1 points; for d = n+1, that's n points. So lines of those three types can cover n points.

- For sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can contain from S_n is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ (let's call m). This is achieved by line y = x (slope 1) and also by line y = x + c? Actually we need to confirm that the maximum is m. Let's prove: For any line L with slope not 0, ∞, -1, consider the integer points on L∩S_n. The line L can be expressed as y = mx + c with m ≠ 0, ∞, -1. The points are integer solutions. The difference in y between two points with integer x is m times the difference in x. For the points to be integer, m must be rational. Write m = p/q in lowest terms. Then the points are spaced by q in x. The number of integer points on L∩S_n is at most something like ⌊(n+1)/q⌋? Not exactly. But we can bound using the fact that a+b is bounded. Another approach: The line L is not parallel to the anti-diagonal, so as a varies, a+b changes linearly with slope (1+m). If m > -1, then a+b increases; if m < -1, a+b decreases. So the line will intersect S_n in a segment. The number of integer points on that segment is at most the number of integer a such that the corresponding b is integer and a+b ≤ n+1. That's like a linear Diophantine equation. The maximum number occurs when m = 1 (since then the line goes through the "dense" part). For m = 1, the integer points are (a,a+c). The condition a ≥ 1, a+c ≥ 1, 2a + c ≤ n+1. The number of such points is something like ⌊(n+1 - c)/2⌋. For c = 0, that's ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For c = 1, it's ⌊n/2⌋, etc. So the maximum is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For other slopes, the number is smaller. So indeed m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Now, we need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines covering S_n, with exactly k sunny lines. Let's denote the set of lines L. We have n lines. Let L_sunny = {L_i} (k of them). Let L_non = {L_j} (n−k). The lines are distinct. The covering condition: S_n ⊆ ⋃_{L} (L∩S_n). The union of the incidences covers all points.

We can attempt to construct configurations with certain k, and also prove that certain k are impossible.

First, note that k = 0 is always possible: take all lines horizontal (y = 1,2,…,n). They are not sunny (since horizontal). That's n lines. They cover all points because each b from 1 to n appears, and for each such b, all a from 1 to n+1-b are covered. So k=0 works.

Now, for k = n, all lines sunny. Then we need to cover S_n with n sunny lines. As argued, if such a configuration exists, then each sunny line must contain exactly m points (since total capacity is n*m, and N = n*m). Moreover, the lines must be disjoint (no overlaps). So we need to find n sunny lines that partition S_n. The only candidate lines that achieve m points are those with slope 1 and offset 0 (i.e., y = x). But is it possible that some other sunny line also has m points? We argued that the maximum is m, and only y = x achieves it. So we need to check whether we can have n lines, all sunny, each with m points. If n is odd, m = (n+1)/2. Then y = x gives m points. So that's one line. To have n lines, we would need n distinct sunny lines, each with m points. Since there is only one such line, impossible. For n even, m = n/2. The line y = x gives (n+2)/2? Actually for n even, n = 2t. Then m = t. The line y = x passes through (a,a) for a=1..t, that's t points. So that's one line. Are there other lines with t points? For slope 2? Not. So impossible. So k = n is impossible for all n ≥ 3. But wait, we found a construction for n=3 with k=3 (all sunny) that works. But according to this argument, for n=3, m = 2, and the line y = x gives 2 points. That's one line. We need 3 lines, all sunny. So if we claim that all sunny lines must achieve m points and be disjoint, then for n=3, we would need three lines each with 2 points, but we only have one such line. But we actually succeeded with three lines: y=x (2 points), line through (2,1)-(1,3) (slope -2, 2 points), line through (3,1)-(1,2) (slope -0.5, 2 points). So those lines are sunny and each has 2 points. So indeed they achieve m points (2). But we said that only y = x achieves m points. But the other two lines also have 2 points, which is m. So they also achieve m points. So our earlier claim that only y = x achieves m points is false for n=3. Let's check: n=3, m=2. The line y=x has points (1,1),(2,2). That's 2. The line through (2,1)-(1,3) has slope (3-1)/(1-2) = 2/-1 = -2. That line passes through (2,1) and (1,3). Are both in S? (2,1) sum=3, (1,3) sum=4, both ≤4. So that's 2 points. For n=3, yes. For n=5, we have m=3. The line y=x gives 3 points. Are there other lines with 3 points? Let's examine. For slope 2, we saw at most 2. For slope 1/2, at most 2. For slope -1 (not sunny). We need to determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) for which there exists a configuration. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. The solution likely involves analyzing the parity and using constructions based on taking lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) to cover many points, and then covering the remaining points with sunny lines. The trick is that the points in S_n form a triangular lattice, and we can use lines that are parallel to the axes or the anti-diagonal to cover many points, leaving a smaller triangle that can be covered by sunny lines. The number of sunny lines we can afford depends on how many points remain. The question: Given n, we have n lines total. If we choose some lines to be non-sunny, we can cover a certain number of points. The remaining points must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines have limited capacity. So we need to find for which k there exists a choice of lines (some sunny, some non-sunny) such that the union covers S_n.

Let’s denote a = number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - a. We have a non-sunny lines. They are each parallel to one of the three forbidden directions. The union of these a lines can cover at most some number of points. To cover all points, we need that the complement (points not on any non-sunny line) is covered by the sunny lines. The complement is a subset of S_n. The sunny lines are k lines, each can cover at most m points. But we can also have sunny lines covering points that are already covered by non-sunny lines; that's okay. So the necessary condition is that the complement size is ≤ k * m (since each sunny line can cover at most m points, but we need to cover all complement points). However, the complement may be large, but we can also use sunny lines that cover points in the complement and also points on non-sunny lines, but the complement must be covered. So we need |R| ≤ k * m. But also note that the sunny lines might not be able to cover all complement points if the complement points are not collinear in sunny directions. But we can try to design the sunny lines to cover the complement efficiently. The bound |R| ≤ k * m is necessary, but not sufficient. However, we can attempt to construct configurations where the complement is exactly a triangle of size something, and we use a partition into sunny lines. The key is to understand the structure of the complement when we take a set of non-sunny lines.

We can choose the non-sunny lines arbitrarily. The maximum number of distinct points that a set of a non-sunny lines can cover is limited. Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. Let's structure:

1. Define S_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^2 : a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1}. |S_n| = N = n(n+1)/2.
2. A line is sunny iff its slope is not 0, ∞, -1.
3. We need to determine all k (0≤k≤n) for which there exists a set L of n distinct lines covering S_n (i.e., S_n ⊆ ⋃_{L∈L} (L∩S_n)).
4. Show that k = 0,1,3 are achievable.
   - For k = 0: take all lines y = 1,…,n (horizontal). Prove they cover S_n.
   - For k = 1: take lines y = 1,…,n-1 (horizontal) and one line L that is sunny and passes through (1,n). Show L can be chosen (e.g., y = x + (n-1) is sunny? But we need to ensure it is sunny. For n≥3, line through (1,n) with slope? Could be vertical? But vertical is not sunny. So we need a sunny line that passes through (1,n). For example, the line with equation y = -x + (n+1) passes through (1,n) and (n,1), but slope -1 is not sunny. So not that. The line y = x + (n-1) passes through (1,n) (since 1 + (n-1) = n). That line has slope 1, which is sunny. Also it passes through (2,n+1) which is not in S_n (since a+b > n+1). So it contains (1,n) only from S_n? It also contains (2,n+1) not in S_n. So it's fine. So L: y = x + (n-1) is sunny. It passes through (1,n). So we can use that. But we need to ensure that this line is distinct from the horizontal lines. Since horizontal lines are y = constant, they are not equal to y = x + (n-1) unless n-1 = constant and slope 0, not. So distinct. So k=1 works. However, we also need to cover all points with the n lines. The union of horizontal lines y=1..n-1 covers all points with b ≤ n-1. The only points with b = n are those with a = 1 (since a+b ≤ n+1 → a ≤ 1). So (1,n) is the only point not covered. The sunny line L passes through (1,n). So covered. Good.
   - For k = 3: we need to construct n lines with exactly 3 sunny lines covering S_n. Construction: take n-3 horizontal lines y = 1,2,…,n-3 (if n-3≥0). So many sunny lines. The question: can we find three sunny lines such that each point is on at least one line, and lines are distinct?
