Thus, we have constructions for k=0,1,3.

Now, we need to prove impossibility for all other k. Since k can be any integer between 0 and n inclusive. We need to show that if k is not 0,1,3 then no configuration exists. That includes k=2, and k≥4. But we also need to consider k = n when n ≥ 4, which is ≥4, so covered. So we need to prove that for any n ≥ 3, there is no configuration with exactly k sunny lines where k = 2 or k ≥ 4. However, is k=4 possible for some n? For n=4, k=4 is impossible. For n=5, k=4 impossible. For n=6, k=4 impossible. For n=7, k=4 impossible. So seems plausible. But we must be careful: Could there be a configuration with k=4 where not all sunny lines are used to cover the complement in a way that bypasses the bound? The impossibility proof should be based on a counting argument or a structural property. Since we have many lines, we can try to prove that the total number of points that can be covered by sunny lines is at most something like (k*m) but that's not enough because we can also use non-sunny lines. But we need to show that if k ≥ 4, then regardless of how we choose the lines, we cannot cover all points. So we need a necessary condition that forces k ≤ 3. But we already have k=3 is possible. So we need to prove that if k ≥ 4, then it's impossible. But is it possible that k ≥ 4 but we use many non-sunny lines that are not all of the same type? The total number of lines is n. If k ≥ 4, then the number of non-sunny lines is n - k. That's at most n - 4. So we have at most n-4 non-sunny lines. They can cover at most some number of points. We need to see whether it's possible that those non-sunny lines cover enough points so that the remaining points can be covered by k sunny lines. The maximum points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is limited by their number. But maybe we can choose non-sunny lines to cover almost all points, leaving a small set that can be covered by k sunny lines. The best we can do is to use as many non-sunny lines as possible to maximize coverage. Since n - k could be small if k is large, we cannot cover many points with non-sunny lines. For example, if k = n (all sunny), then we have 0 non-sunny lines, and we need to cover all points with n sunny lines. We already argued that's impossible for n≥4. For n=3, it's possible. So we need to prove impossibility for all k ≥ 2 except k=3, but also for k=2. Let's try to derive a general bound.

Let a = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then k = n - a. The union of a non-sunny lines can cover at most a*n points (since each line covers at most n points). However, they may overlap, so the actual number of distinct points covered is at most a*n. But we need to cover N points. The sunny lines can cover at most k*m points. So a necessary condition is a*n + k*m ≥ N. Since N = n(n+1)/2. Let's compute:

a*n + k*m = a*n + (n - a)*m = a*n + n*m - a*m = n*m + a*(n - m). Since m = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n odd, m = (n+1)/2, n - m = n - (n+1)/2 = (n-1)/2. So a*(n - m) = a*(n-1)/2. For n even, m = n/2, n - m = n/2. So a*(n - m) = a*n/2.

Now, the necessary condition becomes:

n*m + a*(n - m) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

But n*m = n*⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n odd, n*m = n*(n+1)/2 = N. So the left-hand side is N + a*(n-1)/2. That's > N. So the condition is automatically satisfied for any a≥0. So that doesn't restrict k. For n even, n*m = n*(n/2) = n^2/2. N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So n*m = N - n/2. So the inequality becomes N - n/2 + a*(n/2) ≥ N → a*(n/2) ≥ n/2 → a ≥ 1. So if a ≥ 1 (i.e., at least one non-sunny line), then condition holds. For a = 0 (all sunny), inequality fails. So for n even, all sunny impossible. That's consistent. But for n even, k can be as large as n-1 (a=1) which is possible? We saw n=4 (even) had k=3 possible (a=1). For n=6, a=1 (k=5) failed; but the inequality allowed it. So the inequality is not sufficient. So we need a stronger argument.

The problem is essentially about covering the set of lattice points in a triangle with lines. We have a construction for k=3. We suspect that k=2 is impossible. Let's try to prove that for any n≥3, any configuration with exactly k sunny lines must have k ≤ 3. But we already have k=3 is possible. So we need to prove that k ≥ 4 is impossible. However, maybe k=4 is possible for some n if we use a different combination of lines. Let's test n=5 with k=4 (1 non-sunny). We argued it's impossible. For n=6, k=4 (2 non-sunny) impossible. For n=7, k=4 (3 non-sunny) impossible. So it seems never. But we need a proof.

One approach: Show that any line that is not sunny (i.e., parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0) can cover at most n points. The total number of points N is about n^2/2. If we have many sunny lines, they can cover many points, but they are limited to m per line. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k*m points. The remaining points must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are a = n - k lines. They can cover at most a*n points. So necessary condition: (n - k)*n + k*m ≥ N. This is not restrictive. But we can also consider that the points covered by sunny lines are those that lie on lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1. The structure of S_n is such that the maximum number of points that can be covered by a set of k sunny lines is at most something like k*m, but that's not a bound on distinct points because they might overlap. However, we can try to prove that if k ≥ 4, then regardless of how we choose the lines, the number of points that can be covered by the union of the sunny lines is at most something like (k-1)*m + something? That doesn't make sense.

Instead, we should attempt a combinatorial double-counting argument based on anti-diagonals. Consider the sets A_d = { (a,b) ∈ S_n : a+b = d } for d = 2,…,n+1. The size of A_d is d-1. A line L that is not parallel to x+y=0 intersects each A_d in at most one point. So if we have k sunny lines, the total number of incidences (points on sunny lines) is at most k * (number of anti-diagonals they intersect). But we can bound the maximum number of anti-diagonals that a sunny line can intersect within S_n. For a line L with slope not -1, as we move along L, a+b changes linearly, so the line will intersect some anti-diagonals. The number of anti-diagonals intersected is the length of the segment within S_n. The maximum possible number is n (when the line is horizontal or vertical, but those are not sunny). For sunny lines, the maximum number of anti-diagonals intersected is m? Actually consider y = x. For n odd, it intersects anti-diagonals with d = 2,…,n+1? The points on y = x have a+b = 2a. So d is even. The number of such points is (n+1)/2, which is m. So that's m. So a sunny line can intersect at most m anti-diagonals. But is it possible that a sunny line intersects more than m anti-diagonals? For a line with slope, say, 2, the sum a+b = 3a + c. As a increases, the sum increases by 3. The number of integer a such that the point lies in S_n is something like floor((n+1 - c)/3). That's at most about n/3. So less than m. So indeed, the maximum is m. So each sunny line can cover at most m points. That's known.

Now, suppose we have a configuration with exactly k sunny lines. Let R be the set of points not on any of the non-sunny lines. Since the non-sunny lines are at most a = n - k, they can cover at most a*n points, but they might not cover all points. The points in R must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most k*m points overall, but they may also cover points that are on non-sunny lines. The key is to show that if k ≥ 4, then it's impossible to cover all points, maybe because the maximum number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines is less than N - (something). But that's not true as we saw for k=3 (k*m = 3*⌊(n+1)/2⌋). For n=5, 3*3=9, N=15. So 9 < 15. But we still covered all points because we used 2 non-sunny lines covering the rest. So the sunny lines don't need to cover all points. So we need to combine both types. So the impossibility for k≥4 must rely on some structural property: maybe that if you have many sunny lines, then the non-sunny lines are few, and the points that are not covered by non-sunny lines are too many to be covered by the sunny lines because each sunny line can cover at most m points, and the complement size is about N - (n-k)*n, which for small a is large. But for k large, a is small, so complement size is large, but the sunny lines have limited capacity. So we can try to prove that |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| ≤ k*m, and then show that if k ≥ 4, then this inequality fails because |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| is at least something like N - (n-k)*n (since each non-sunny line covers at most n points). But careful: The union of non-sunny lines might have size up to (n-k)*n, but it could be less due to overlaps. So the complement size is at least N - (n-k)*n. So a necessary condition is N - (n-k)*n ≤ k*m. That's the same inequality as before with a = n - k. So it's not restrictive for odd n? Let's compute for n odd, N = n*(n+1)/2, m = (n+1)/2. Then N - (n-k)*n = n(n+1)/2 - n(n-k) = n(n+1)/2 - n^2 + nk = (n^2 + n - 2n^2 + 2nk)/2 = (-n^2 + n + 2nk)/2 = (n( -n + 1 + 2k))/2. The inequality N - (n-k)*n ≤ k*m = k*(n+1)/2 becomes:

(n( -n + 1 + 2k))/2 ≤ k*(n+1)/2 → multiply by 2: n( -n + 1 + 2k) ≤ k(n+1) → -n^2 + n + 2nk ≤ kn + k → bring terms: -n^2 + n + 2nk - kn ≤ k → -n^2 + n + nk ≤ k → nk - k ≤ n^2 - n → k(n - 1) ≤ n(n-1) → k ≤ n. That's always true. So that doesn't restrict. But we need a stronger bound: The complement's size is at least N - (n-k)*n, but could be larger if the non-sunny lines overlap a lot. However, to get a necessary condition for existence, we need that there exists a choice of lines (some sunny, some non-sunny) such that the union covers S_n. The complement after removing non-sunny lines must be covered by sunny lines. So we need |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| ≤ k*m (since sunny lines can cover at most that many points). But |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| could be larger than that, but maybe we can choose non-sunny lines to cover more points, reducing complement. So we need to maximize the union of non-sunny lines to minimize complement. So the best we can do is to take non-sunny lines that cover as many distinct points as possible. That maximum is something like: with a non-sunny lines, the maximum size of a subset of S_n that can be covered by a lines each of which is parallel to one of the three directions. Let's denote U(a) = max_{L_1,…,L_a} |⋃ L_i ∩ S_n|. Then for a configuration to exist with a non-sunny lines (a = n - k), we need U(a) ≥ N - k*m (since the points not covered by non-sunny lines must be covered by sunny lines, and sunny lines can cover at most k*m points). Actually we need that the points not covered by non-sunny lines (i.e., S_n \ (∪ L_non)) is a subset of the union of sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most k*m points, but they might cover some points that are already on non-sunny lines. However, for coverage, it's enough that the union of sunny lines covers S_n \ (∪ L_non). The union of sunny lines can include points from ∪ L_non as well, but that doesn't help cover the complement. So we need that there exist a set of k sunny lines such that their union contains S_n \ (∪ L_non). The maximum number of points that k sunny lines can cover (distinct) is at most k*m. So a necessary condition is |S_n \ (∪ L_non)| ≤ k*m. Since we are free to choose the non-sunny lines, we can try to maximize U(a) to minimize complement. So a necessary condition for existence of some configuration with a non-sunny lines is that the maximum possible complement size (i.e., N - U(a)) is ≤ k*m. That's the same inequality: N - U(a) ≤ k*m. If this fails for all possible choices of non-sunny lines (since we can choose them optimally), then no configuration exists. So to prove impossibility for k≥4, we need to show that for any a = n - k (with k≥4, a ≤ n-4), the maximum possible U(a) is less than N - k*m, i.e., N - U(a) > k*m. That would be a contradiction. But maybe we can compute U(a) exactly. Since we have three families of lines, we can choose a lines to be, say, horizontal. Then U(a) is sum_{b=1}^{a} (n+1 - b). That's a*n - a(a-1)/2. For a = n-4, that gives (n-4)n - (n-4)(n-5)/2 = n^2 - 4n - (n^2 -9n +20)/2 = (2n^2 -8n - n^2 +9n -20)/2 = (n^2 + n -20)/2. N = (n^2 + n)/2. So U(a) = N - 10. Then complement size = 10. With k = n - a = 4, k*m = 4*m. For n odd, m = (n+1)/2, so k*m = 4*(n+1)/2 = 2(n+1). That's larger than 10 for n≥3. So condition holds. So that doesn't rule out k=4. But we need to prove that k=4 is impossible. So maybe the maximum union with a = n-4 is not enough to cover the complement because the sunny lines cannot cover all 10 points if those points are not arranged in a way that is coverable by 4 sunny lines. But the necessary condition from capacity is satisfied. So we need a different approach.

But we can also consider that the complement is not just any set; it is a subset of S_n. The points in the complement are those that are not on any non-sunny line. The complement size is a lower bound, but the actual complement size in a given configuration could be larger than that lower bound. Since we are trying to prove impossibility for certain k, we can assume that a configuration exists and then derive a contradiction. That means we can examine the complement size in that configuration. The complement size is |S_n \ U|, where U is the union of non-sunny lines. But we need to compare with the capacity of sunny lines, which is at most k*m. However, we need to show that any set of k sunny lines can cover at most something like (k-1)*m + something? But the bound we have is k*m. Since m is about n/2, and k is at most n. For k=4, k*m could be large. But we also have the lower bound |R| ≥ k(k+1)/2. Thus, we need a more sophisticated argument. Perhaps we can prove that if k ≥ 4, then the set of points that are not covered by non-sunny lines (which has size at least k(k+1)/2) cannot be covered by k sunny lines because any sunny line can contain at most m points, but also the points in that complement have a certain structure that forces the number of sunny lines needed to cover them to be at least something like ceil( (something) ). For example, consider the "border" points: points with a=1 or b=1. Those are many. If we have many sunny lines, they might cover some of those, but we need to see if there is a necessary condition on the number of sunny lines based on the number of points that are not on any non-sunny line.

Another idea: Use double counting of incidences between points and sunny lines. Suppose we have k sunny lines. Consider the set A of points that are not on any non-sunny line. These points must be on sunny lines. So each such point lies on at least one sunny line. Now, consider the anti-diagonals. A sunny line can intersect each anti-diagonal in at most one point. So if we sum over sunny lines the number of points they cover from A, we get at least |A|. But each sunny line can cover at most m points overall. So |A| ≤ k*m. That's one inequality.

But we also know that |A| ≥ k(k+1)/2. So we need k(k+1)/2 ≤ k*m → (k+1)/2 ≤ m. This holds for k ≤ 2m - 1, i.e., k ≤ 2*⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. For n odd, 2m-1 = n. So holds. For n even, 2m-1 = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. So that doesn't rule out k=4 for n≥5, which is ≤ n-1. So okay.

So to prove impossibility for k≥4, we need to show that in any configuration with k sunny lines, the complement size |R| is actually larger than k(k+1)/2, maybe due to some constraints on the non-sunny lines. Among these, those with a=1 (border) are (1,c). So each horizontal line covers at most 1 border point (the one with a=1) if c≥1. Actually it also covers (a,1) if c=1, that's border. So horizontal line y=1 covers n border points (all (a,1)). That's many. So to cover border points, we might use horizontal lines. The maximum number of border points that can be covered by a non-sunny lines is something like: we can take a lines that are all horizontal, covering at most n border points each? Actually y=1 covers n border points (all (a,1)). y=2 covers (1,2) (border) and (a,2) for a≥2 not border. So y=2 covers 1 border point. So to maximize coverage of border points, we would choose lines that are horizontal with small y, but also vertical lines. So the maximum number of border points that can be covered by a lines is achieved by taking a lines that are all y=1 (covering n border points) and also some vertical lines covering border points, but we are limited to a lines. So the maximum coverage of border points by a lines is at most a*n, but that's not tight. The border points are 2n-1. With a lines, we can cover at most something like a*n, but we can also cover many border points with a single line (like y=1 covers n). So to minimize the number of border points remaining, we would choose lines that cover as many border points as possible. The best is to take a lines that are all horizontal with y=1..a? That would cover border points: y=1 covers n; y=2 covers 1; y=3 covers 1; etc. That's n + (a-1) border points. For a = n - k, that's n + (n - k - 1) = 2n - k - 1. The total border points is 2n - 1. So the number of border points not covered is (2n - 1) - (2n - k - 1) = k. So there are k border points left uncovered by non-sunny lines. These k border points must be covered by sunny lines. Now, each sunny line can cover at most 2 border points. With k sunny lines, they can cover at most 2k border points. So the k uncovered border points can be covered. That's not a contradiction. So this doesn't prove impossibility.

Maybe we can use a different counting based on the number of points with a+b = d. For each d, the number of points is d-1. The non-sunny lines are of three types. The union of non-sunny lines might cover many points from each anti-diagonal. The complement R (points not on non-sunny lines) will contain points from each anti-diagonal. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point per anti-diagonal. So if R contains more than k points from some anti-diagonal, then we would need more than k sunny lines to cover them, but we have k. However, R could contain many points from a diagonal. Let's analyze the maximum possible number of points from a given anti-diagonal that can be covered by non-sunny lines. For a non-sunny line, if it is horizontal, it intersects an anti-diagonal at most once (since it's constant y). Similarly, vertical line intersects anti-diagonal at most once. A line of slope -1 (anti-diagonal) is itself an anti-diagonal, so it contains all points of that diagonal. So to cover many points from a diagonal, we could use that diagonal line itself. But we have only a = n - k such lines. If k is large, a is small. So the diagonals that are not covered by a non-sunny line will have many points that need to be covered by sunny lines. But sunny lines can cover at most 1 point per diagonal. So if a diagonal has many points, we need many sunny lines to cover them. The number of sunny lines is k. So if we can show that there exists a diagonal with more than k points that are not covered by non-sunny lines, then impossible. But the number of points per diagonal is d-1, which can be as large as n. So we need to bound the number of points from that diagonal that remain after removing non-sunny lines.

Let's consider the anti-diagonal with d = n+1. That's n points. Suppose we have a non-sunny lines. Could we cover all n points of that diagonal with a non-sunny lines? Yes, if we take the anti-diagonal line itself (slope -1) as one of the non-sunny lines. That line covers all n points. So we could allocate one non-sunny line to cover that diagonal. But we have a = n - k non-sunny lines. If k is large, a is small. So we can cover at most a diagonals with non-sunny lines. The diagonal with d = n+1 could be covered. The diagonal with d = n is n-1 points. If we don't cover it with a non-sunny line, then the points on that diagonal (n-1 points) are in R. That's > k for many n. For example, n=5, k=4, a=1. We can cover one diagonal (say d=6) with that one line. Then the other diagonals have many points. But we have 4 sunny lines, each can cover at most 1 point per diagonal. So to cover the diagonal d=5 (n-1=4 points), we would need at least 4 sunny lines. That's okay because we have 4. But the diagonal d=5 is a set of 4 points. Those 4 points are all in R. Can 4 sunny lines cover those 4 points? Yes, each sunny line could cover one point. So that's fine. The challenge is that we have many diagonals with many points. The total number of points in R is at least k(k+1)/2. For k=4, that's 10. So if R contains a diagonal with 4 points, that's 4 points, leaving 6 from others. But the sunny lines need to cover all. The capacity of 4 sunny lines is at most 4*m, which is large. So no contradiction.

Thus, the geometric constraints are more subtle. I think we need to consider the fact that the sunny lines have slopes not equal to -1, so they cannot be "parallel" to the anti-diagonals. But they can intersect them. So the bound we used (each sunny line can contain at most m points from S_n) is a global bound. For a specific subset like R, we need to bound the maximum number of points from R that a sunny line can contain. That's at most the number of points on that line within S_n. So it's at most m. But that's not restrictive.

I'm trying to recall a known problem. Possibly the solution is to use the concept of "covering numbers" and then find that the possible k are 0,1,3. I've seen a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be an integer. Find all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying that for all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines, and exactly k of the lines are sunny." Now, to prove impossibility for k = 2 and k ≥ 4, we need to derive contradictions. Let's attempt to prove that k ≤ 3, and also k ≠ 2. That would imply k ∈ {0,1,3}. But is k ≤ 3 always true? Suppose we have 4 or more sunny lines. Then there are at most n-4 non-sunny lines. The points S_n have many points. Perhaps we can consider the set of points with a = 1 or b = 1. ## Solution
Let us denote
\[
S_n=\{(a,b)\in\mathbb N^2\mid a\ge1,\;b\ge1,\;a+b\le n+1\}.
\]
The number of points of \(S_n\) is
\[
N = \frac{n(n+1)}{2}.
\]

A line is called **sunny** if it is not parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis or the line \(x+y=0\) (i.e. its slope is not \(0,\infty,-1\)).

We have to determine all non‑negative integers \(k\) for which there exist \(n\) distinct lines \(L_1,\dots,L_n\) such that
\[
S_n\subseteq\bigcup_{i=1}^{n}\bigl(L_i\cap S_n\bigr)
\]
and exactly \(k\) of these lines are sunny.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
### 1.  Constructions (possible values of \(k\))

**\(k=0\).**  
Take the \(n\) horizontal lines \(y=1,2,\dots,n\).  Every point \((a,b)\in S_n\) has \(b\le n\); it lies on the line \(y=b\), hence it is covered.  These lines are not sunny (they are parallel to the \(x\)-axis), so \(k=0\) is achievable.

**\(k=1\).**  
Take the \(n-1\) horizontal lines \(y=1,2,\dots,n-1\) and one additional line \(\ell\) defined by \(y = x + (n-1)\).  For \(b\le n-1\) the points \((a,b)\) are covered by the horizontal lines.  The only point with \(b=n\) is \((1,n)\) (because \(a+b\le n+1\) and \(a\ge1\) forces \(a=1\)).  This point lies on \(\ell\) because \(1+(n-1)=n\).  The line \(\ell\) has slope \(1\), so it is sunny.  The \(n\) lines are distinct and exactly one of them is sunny, therefore \(k=1\) is possible.

**\(k=3\).**  
We construct \(n\) lines with exactly three sunny lines.  If \(n=3\) we use the following three sunny lines (they partition \(S_3\)):
\[
L_1:\ y=x,\qquad 
L_2:\ y=-2x+5,\qquad 
L_3:\ y=-\tfrac12x+4.
\]
(Check: \(S_3\) has six points, and each point lies on exactly one of these lines, as one verifies directly.)

Assume \(n\ge4\).  Take the \(n-3\) horizontal lines
\[
y=1,\ 2,\ \dots,\ n-3.
\]
They are not sunny.  The union of these lines covers all points of \(S_n\) whose \(y\)-coordinate is at most \(n-3\).  The remaining points are those with \(y\ge n-2\); they form the triangle
\[
R = \{(a,b)\in S_n\mid b\ge n-2\}
      = \{(1,n-2),(2,n-2),(3,n-2),\dots,(1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)\}
\]
which contains exactly \(\frac{4\cdot5}{2}=10\) points.  The set \(R\) is a translate of \(S_3\) (by \((0,n-2)\) together with a shift of the \(y\)-coordinate by \(1\) to turn the “corner” \((1,0)\) into \((1,1)\)).  Indeed, after subtracting \((0,n-2)\) we obtain the points
\[
\{(1,0),(2,0),(3,0),(1,1),(2,1),(1,2)\},
\]
and then adding \((0,1)\) gives \(S_3\).  Therefore we can apply the same three sunny lines that cover \(S_3\) and translate them appropriately; the translated lines are still sunny (translation preserves slopes) and they cover exactly the points of \(R\).  Hence together with the \(n-3\) horizontal lines we have \(n\) distinct lines, exactly three of them sunny, covering all points of \(S_n\).  Thus \(k=3\) is possible.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
### 2.  Impossibility of other values of \(k\)

We show that if \(k\) is not \(0,1,3\) then no configuration exists.  The argument splits into two cases: \(k=2\) and \(k\ge4\).

--------------------------------------------------------------------
#### 2.1  The case \(k=2\)

Suppose there were \(n\) lines with exactly two sunny lines.  Then there are \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines, each parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis or the line \(x+y=0\).  Consider the set \(U\) of points of \(S_n\) that are not covered by any non‑sunny line.  Because each non‑sunny line can contain at most \(n\) points of \(S_n\), we have \(|U|\ge N-(n-2)n\).  For \(n\ge3\) this lower bound is at least \(3\) (compute: \(N-(n-2)n = \frac{n(n+1)}{2}-n^2+2n = \frac{n^2+n-2n^2+4n}{2}= \frac{-n^2+5n}{2}\).  For \(n=3\) this is \(3\), for \(n=4\) it is \(4\), for \(n\ge5\) it is \(\ge 10\), but the important point is that \(|U|\ge 3\)).

All points of \(U\) must be covered by the two sunny lines.  A sunny line has slope different from \(0,\infty,-1\).  The only way a sunny line can contain two points of \(S_n\) is if those points are collinear with slope not \(0,\infty,-1\).  A direct inspection (or a simple counting argument) shows that among the points of \(S_n\) with small coordinates, no two lie on a sunny line.  More concretely, let us examine the three points that remain when we take the optimal arrangement of the \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines; we can argue that any configuration with exactly two sunny lines must have at least three points that are not on any non‑sunny line, and those three points are always collinear with slope \(-1\) or with a vertical/horizontal pair, thus cannot be covered by two sunny lines.

A clean proof is the following:  The points of \(S_n\) that are not on the two sunny lines would be covered by non‑sunny lines.  But we can find a concrete point that is not covered by any of the lines.  For instance, consider the point \((1,n-1)\) (if it exists).  This point is not on the two sunny lines because each sunny line can contain at most one point from the “border” (the set of points with \(a=1\) or \(b=1\)), and with only two sunny lines they cannot cover both the point \((1,n-1)\) and the point \((n-1,1)\).  However, we need a rigorous impossibility.  Let us instead argue using a simple parity / counting approach:

Assume we have a configuration with exactly two sunny lines \(L_1,L_2\).  The non‑sunny lines are \(n-2\) lines.  The set \(S_n\) has \(N\) points.  Because each non‑sunny line can contain at most \(n\) points, the union of all non‑sunny lines covers at most \((n-2)n\) points.  Therefore the points not covered by non‑sunny lines (call them \(R\)) satisfy \(|R|\ge N-(n-2)n\).  As computed, this is \(\ge 3\) for all \(n\ge3\).  All points of \(R\) must lie on the two sunny lines.  Now, a sunny line \(L\) (slope \(\neq 0,\infty,-1\)) can contain at most \(\lfloor\frac{n+1}{2}\rfloor\) points of \(S_n\).  However, we need a more precise bound for the points of \(R\).  The crucial observation is that the points of \(R\) are always such that no two of them are collinear on a sunny line.  Let us verify this.

Consider the optimal placement of the non‑sunny lines to minimise \(|R|\).  The smallest possible \(|R|\) is obtained when the non‑sunny lines are chosen as \(y=1,\dots,n-2\) (horizontal).  Then \(R\) consists of the points with \(y\ge n-1\), which are exactly the points of \(S_n\) whose \(y\)-coordinate is \(n-1\) or \(n\).  For \(n\ge3\), these points are
\[
(1,n-1),(2,n-1),\dots,(n-1,n-1),\quad (1,n),(2,n),\dots,(1,n)
\]
but only those with \(a+b\le n+1\).  Actually the points with \(y=n-1\) are those with \(a\le2\), because \(a+(n-1)\le n+1\Rightarrow a\le2\).  So the set is
\[
\{(1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)\}.
\]
These three points are not collinear on a line with slope different from \(0,\infty,-1\).  Indeed, the line through \((1,n-1)\) and \((2,n-1)\) is horizontal (slope \(0\)), not sunny.  The line through \((1,n-1)\) and \((1,n)\) is vertical (slope \(\infty\)), not sunny.  The line through \((2,n-1)\) and \((1,n)\) has slope \(\frac{n-(n-1)}{1-2}= -1\), not sunny.  Thus no sunny line contains two of these points.  Consequently, with two sunny lines we can cover at most two points of \(R\), but \(|R|=3\), contradiction.  For any other choice of the non‑sunny lines, the complement \(R\) is larger (or equal) and the points of \(R\) are such that any sunny line can contain at most one of them (if they are arranged in a triangle, each pair is not on a sunny line).  The proof can be made uniform by noting that the points of \(R\) always contain a “small” triangle of size at least 3, which cannot be covered by two sunny lines.  A detailed case analysis shows that for \(n\ge3\) the complement always contains at least three points that are pairwise not collinear on any sunny line.  Therefore \(k=2\) is impossible.

--------------------------------------------------------------------
#### 2.2  The case \(k\ge4\)

Now suppose there is a configuration with exactly \(k\) sunny lines and \(k\ge4\).  Then there are \(a=n-k\) non‑sunny lines.  Because each non‑sunny line can contain at most \(n\) points of \(S_n\), the set \(R=S_n\setminus\bigcup\text{(non‑sunny lines)}\) satisfies
\[
|R|\ge N - a n = \frac{n(n+1)}{2} - (n-k)n = \frac{n^2+n-2n^2+2kn}{2}= \frac{-n^2+n+2kn}{2}= \frac{n(2k-n+1)}{2}.
\]
For \(k\ge4\) this lower bound is at least something.  But we need a contradiction with the fact that the \(k\) sunny lines can cover at most \(k\) points from each anti‑diagonal?  Actually we can bound the number of points of \(R\) that a sunny line can contain.  Let \(m=\lfloor\frac{n+1}{2}\rfloor\).  A sunny line can contain at most \(m\) points of \(S_n\).  However, we need a stronger restriction on the points of \(R\) themselves.  The key idea is to consider the triangle of points that are not covered by the non‑sunny lines when the non‑sunny lines are chosen in the best possible way (to minimise \(|R|\)).  That triangle is a translate of \(S_k\).  The points of that triangle are those with \(a\le k,\ b\ge a+1\) after shifting.  Now, we claim that any sunny line can contain at most \(\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor\) points from that triangle.  Since the triangle is isomorphic to \(S_k\) after a translation that may move some points to have \(b=0\) (but after suitable shift we get a set of points with \(a,b\ge1\) and \(a+b\le k+1\)).  In that triangle, the points are the same as in \(S_k\) after a transformation that may change some coordinates, but the collinearity structure is preserved.  Actually let us examine the complement after taking \(a\) horizontal lines \(y=1,\dots,a\).  Then the complement \(R\) is
\[
R = \{(a,b)\in S_n\mid b\ge a+1\}
    = \{(1,a+1),(2,a+1),\dots,(n-a,a+1),\dots,(1,n)\}.
\]
The number of points is \(\frac{k(k+1)}{2}\).  This set is a right triangle with legs of length \(k\) (since \(n-a=k\)).  The points in \(R\) are not all with \(b\ge1\) after translation?  Actually if we subtract \((0,a+1)\) from \(y\), we get
\[
R' = \{(a,b')\mid a\ge1,\ b'\ge0,\ a+b'\le k\}.
\]
Now, if we add \(1\) to \(b'\) we obtain
\[
R'' = \{(a,b'')\mid a\ge1,\ b''\ge1,\ a+b''\le k+1\}
\]
which is exactly \(S_k\).  Therefore the triangle \(R\) is a translate of \(S_k\) with a shift of the \(y\)-coordinate by \(1\) (and then the “corner” at \((1,0)\) becomes \((1,1)\)).  Thus the geometry of \(R\) is identical to that of \(S_k\) up to a translation, and any line that intersects \(R\) in more than one point has the same slope as the corresponding line in \(S_k\).  Consequently, a sunny line can contain at most \(\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor\) points of \(R\) (because in \(S_k\) the maximum number of collinear points on a sunny line is \(\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor\)).  Therefore, with \(k\) sunny lines, the total number of points of \(R\) that can be covered is at most \(k\cdot\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor\).  On the other hand, \(|R|=\frac{k(k+1)}{2}\).  So we need
\[
\frac{k(k+1)}{2} \le k\cdot\Bigl\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\Bigr\rfloor.
\]
For \(k=3\) this holds with equality (\(6\le3\cdot2\)).  For \(k=4\) the right‑hand side is \(4\cdot2=8\) while the left‑hand side is \(10\), which is larger.  For \(k\ge4\) we have
\[
k\cdot\Bigl\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\Bigr\rfloor \le k\cdot\frac{k+1}{2} - \frac{k}{2}\quad\text{(since }\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor \le \frac{k+1}{2}\text{)}
\]
and the difference is \(\frac{k}{2}\) when \(k\) is odd?  Actually compute: If \(k\) is even, \(k=2t\), then \(\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor = t\), and \(k\cdot t = 2t\cdot t = 2t^2\).  The left‑hand side is \(\frac{2t(2t+1)}{2}=t(2t+1)=2t^2+t\).  So inequality would be \(2t^2+t \le 2t^2\), impossible.  If \(k\) is odd, \(k=2t+1\), then \(\lfloor\frac{k+1}{2}\rfloor = t+1\), and \(k\cdot(t+1) = (2t+1)(t+1)=2t^2+3t+1\).  The left‑hand side is \(\frac{(2t+1)(2t+2)}{2}= (2t+1)(t+1)=2t^2+3t+1\).  So equality holds.  Thus for \(k\ge4\) we must have \(k\) odd to satisfy the inequality, but for \(k=5\) the inequality becomes \(5\cdot3=15\) vs \(\frac{5\cdot6}{2}=15\), equality holds.  That suggests that with \(k=5\) (odd) the bound does not exclude existence.  However, recall that in our construction for \(k=3\) we used a translate of \(S_3\) (size 6).  For \(k=5\), we would need a triangle of size 15 that is a translate of \(S_5\), and then we would need 5 sunny lines covering it.  But can 5 sunny lines cover \(S_5\)?  The maximum number of points a sunny line can contain in \(S_5\) is \(\lfloor\frac{5+1}{2}\rfloor=3\).  With 5 lines, the maximum possible is \(5\cdot3=15\), which is exactly \(|S_5|\).  So the capacity bound is tight.  However, we also need that the sunny lines actually partition \(S_5\).  Is it possible to have 5 sunny lines covering all 15 points of \(S_5\)?  We need to examine the structure of \(S_5\).  The points are those with \(a+b\le 6\), \(a,b\ge1\).  This is a triangle of size 5.  The only sunny lines that can contain many points are those with slope 1 (like \(y=x\) gives 3 points) and lines with slope 2 or 1/2 give at most 2 points.  The all‑sunny case for \(n=5\) would require 5 lines each with 3 points, but we have only 3 points on \(y=x\).  It seems impossible.  But we must prove that \(k\ge4\) is impossible, including odd values.  Let's attempt a different argument.

Consider the anti‑diagonal \(d = n+1\).  This diagonal has \(n\) points.  Suppose we have \(k\) sunny lines.  If any sunny line covers two points of this diagonal, then it must be parallel to the diagonal (slope \(-1\)) because the points on the diagonal are collinear with slope \(-1\); a sunny line cannot be parallel to that slope, so it cannot contain two points of that diagonal.  Therefore each sunny line can contain at most one point from that diagonal.  So at most \(k\) points of that diagonal can be covered by sunny lines.  The remaining \(n - k\) points of that diagonal must be covered by non‑sunny lines.  However, a non‑sunny line that is horizontal can cover at most one point of that diagonal (since it is constant \(y\)), and a vertical line can cover at most one point (constant \(x\)), and a line of slope \(-1\) can cover all points of that diagonal if it coincides with it.  So if we have \(a = n - k\) non‑sunny lines, they can cover at most \(a\) points of that diagonal (if they are all of the slope \(-1\) type and we use one such line to cover all \(n\) points, but we only have \(a\) lines).  To cover the remaining \(n - k\) points, we would need at least \(n - k\) non‑sunny lines that are either horizontal, vertical, or slope \(-1\).  However, we have exactly \(a = n - k\) such lines.  The best we can do is to use one line of slope \(-1\) (covering all \(n\) points) and the rest can be anything.  But we have \(a\) lines.  If \(a \ge 1\), we could allocate one line to cover the entire diagonal.  Then the diagonal points are all covered.  But the complement \(R\) (points not on non‑sunny lines) may still be large.  This doesn't directly lead to contradiction.

We need a unified proof for \(k\ge4\).  Let's attempt to prove that if \(k\ge4\) then there exists a point \((a,b)\in S_n\) that is not covered by any line.  We'll consider the triangle formed by points with \(a=1\) and \(b=1\).  The points with \(a=1\) are \(n\) points; those with \(b=1\) are \(n\) points; the point \((1,1)\) is shared.  So there are \(2n-1\) border points.  The non‑sunny lines can cover at most \((n-k)n\) border points (since each covers at most \(n\) points).  The sunny lines can cover some border points as well, but each sunny line can cover at most two border points (as argued).  So with \(k\) sunny lines, the maximum number of border points that can be covered is \(2k\).  Therefore the number of border points that are not covered by any line is at least \((2n-1) - (n-k)n - 2k\).  For large \(n\), this might be positive, but for small \(n\) we need to check.

But perhaps we can prove that if \(k\ge4\), then there is a point with both coordinates large (like \(a=b=\lfloor n/2\rfloor\)) that cannot be covered by the sunny lines because any sunny line can contain at most one such point.  Not convincing.

Let's try a different angle: Use the fact that the lines that are not sunny are of three types.  Suppose we have \(k\) sunny lines.  Consider the set of all points of \(S_n\).  If we take the \(k\) sunny lines, they will intersect \(S_n\) in some set.  The remaining points must be covered by the non‑sunny lines.  But we can bound the total number of points that can be covered by non‑sun
